Chapter on disliked matters. The sale of human waste is disliked, the ruling on selling dung and animal droppings. Section on sale. He said: There is no harm in selling dung, and the sale of human waste is disliked. Al-Shafi'i said: The sale of dung is also not permissible because it is impure by essence, so it resembles human waste and the hide of a dead animal before tanning. Our proof is that it is beneficial, because it is thrown onto lands to increase produce, so it is property, and property is subject to sale, unlike human waste because it is only beneficial when mixed. And the sale of mixed human waste is permissible; this is narrated from Muhammad and it is correct. Similarly, it is not permissible to benefit from unmixed human waste, but mixed is permissible in the correct opinion. Mixed human waste is like olive oil mixed with impurity. Translation: Section on sale. Muhammad said: There is no harm in selling dung, and the sale of human waste is disliked. Al-Shafi'i said: The sale of dung is also not permissible because it is impure by essence, so it resembles human waste and the hide of a dead animal before tanning. Our proof is that benefit is derived from it because it is thrown onto lands to increase produce, so it becomes property, and property is subject to sale, unlike human waste because benefit is derived from it only when mixed. And the sale of mixed human waste is permissible; this is narrated from Muhammad and it is correct. Similarly, benefit from mixed human waste is permissible, not from unmixed, according to the correct opinion. Mixed human waste is like olive oil mixed with impurity. Commentary: The sale of human waste is disliked because it is impure by essence and also because it is mixed with soil or ash and fertilizer and thrown onto lands. The sale of dung etc. is permissible. Imam al-Shafi'i considered it impermissible; he analogized dung to human waste and to the hide of a dead animal before tanning. Our proof is that it is property because it is pure property that is thrown onto land to strengthen it, and all property is permissible to sell. Therefore, the sale of dung and animal droppings is permissible. Benefit from human waste is obtained when it is mixed, and the sale of mixed human waste is permissible, as narrated from Imam Muhammad, and this is correct. The ruling of mixed human waste is like olive oil mixed with impurity, in that benefit from it is permissible, so its sale is also permissible. Note: Benefit from pure human waste is permissible? Then its sale would also be permissible. Majma' al-Anhur p. 524 vol. 2.
Source pages p. 367

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools regarding the sale of animal dung (سرقين) and human waste (عذرة). The Hanafi position, stated by Imam Muhammad in al-Jami' al-Saghir, is that selling dung is permissible (لا بأس) while selling human waste is disliked (يكره). The Shafi'i school holds that even dung is impermissible because it is najis al-ayn (impure by essence). The Hanafi proof (dalil) is that dung is useful wealth (مال منتفع به) since it is used as fertilizer to increase crop yield, and wealth is the subject of sale. Human waste is different: it is only beneficial when mixed with soil or other substances; in its pure form it is not beneficial, hence its sale is disliked. However, when human waste is mixed with earth, its sale becomes permissible, and this is the correct view narrated from Imam Muhammad. The analogy is made to olive oil mixed with impurity—the mixture becomes usable. The passage also includes a note (تنبیہ) that according to some later authorities (like al-Ayni and al-Zabidi), pure human waste may also be permissible to use and sell, but the relied-upon position is the one given in the matn.

Key Terms
سرقين (sarqīn): Animal dung, used as fertilizer.
عذرة ('adhirah): Human waste/excrement.
نجس العين (najis al-'ayn): Impure by essence; the substance itself is considered impure regardless of quantity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is selling dung (سرقين) permissible while selling pure human waste (عذرة) is disliked?
A) Because dung is less impure than human waste
B) Because dung is useful in itself as fertilizer and considered wealth, while human waste is only useful when mixed
C) Because the Shafi'i school allows dung sale
D) Because dung is not najis at all

Key Takeaway
Dung is permissible to sell because it is beneficial property; human waste is only permissible when mixed with earth.

Acceptance of a Single Person's Statement in Transactions
Ruling on relying on one person's claim in buying a slave girl

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on relying on a single person's statement in transactions and apply the condition of dominant presumption (ghalabat al-zann) to determine permissibility.

Core Idea
In transactions, need relaxes evidence: one truthful-seeming informant suffices.

In daily transactions, we often rely on a single person's word without formal witnesses. Islamic law accommodates this practical need. The Hanafi school, in particular, allows acting on the statement of a single person in mu'amalat, provided the hearer has a dominant presumption of truthfulness. This lesson examines the ruling, its conditions, and its application to a classic case: buying a slave girl from someone claiming to be the owner's agent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is relying on a single person's statement in a transaction. The Hanafi ruling is that it is permissible (ja'iz) under certain conditions.
This is distinct from testimony (shahadah), which requires multiple witnesses.
2
Determine the condition of the informant
The informant must be sane; gender, religion, and justice are irrelevant. If the informant is trustworthy (thiqah), his word is accepted directly. If not, the buyer must assess: if his dominant presumption (ghalabat al-zann) is that the informant is truthful, he may act.
Ghalabat al-zann is given the weight of certainty in worldly matters.
3
Identify the exception
If the buyer's dominant presumption is that the informant is lying, it is not permissible to act on the statement. Dominant presumption of falsehood is treated as certainty in preventing action.
This is based on the legal maxim: 'al-ghalib yu'zalu bi al-yaqin.'
4
Apply to the case of buying a slave girl
A man sees a slave girl being sold by someone who claims to be the owner's agent. If the buyer has a dominant presumption of truthfulness (directly if the seller is thiqah, or after investigation), he may buy the slave and even have intercourse with her. The single statement suffices.
This example illustrates the practical application of the principle.

Source Text

تشریح ماقبل میں گذر چکا ہے کہ معاملات میں ایک آدمی کا قول مقبول ہوتا ہے بس اس کا میز عاقل ہونا کافی ہے خواہ وہ مرد ہو یا عورت مسلمان ہو یا کفر عادل ہو یا فاسق ، لہذا جب دیکھا کہ زید منڈی میں کسی باندی کو بیچ رہا ہے اور عمر جانتا ہے کہ یہ باندی خالد کی ہے لیکن زید کہتا ہے کہ خالد نے مجھے اس کے بیچنے کا وکیل بنایا ہے کہتا ہے کہ میں نے خالد سے اس کو خرید لیا ہے یا کہتا ہے کہ خالد نے اس کو مجھے ہبہ کر دی ہے یا صدقہ میں دیدی ہے تو ان تمام صورتوں میں عمرو کے لئے جائز ہے کہ زید کی بات پر اعتماد کرے اور باندی کو خرید لے اور اس سے ولی کرے کیونکہ یہ مخبر عقل و دین رکھتا ہے جس میں جھوٹ بولنا حرام ہے تو یہ خبر صحیح ہے اور اس کا کوئی معارض بھی نہیں اور یہ معاملہ ہے جس میں ایک آدمی کا قول مقبول ہے۔ محمد یوسف نحفر له

وهذا إذ كان ثقة وكذا اذا كان غير ثقة و اكبر رائه انه صادق لان عدالة المخبر في المعاملات غير لازمة للحاجة على مامر
ترجمہ اور یہ (اس کے قول کو قبول کرنا ) جب ہے جبکہ وہ ثقہ ہو اور ایسے ہی جبکہ وہ غیر ثقہ ہو اور اس کا غالب گمان یہ ہو کہ یہ سچا ہے اسلئے کہ معاملات میں مخبر کی عدالت لازم نہیں ہے حاجت کی وجہ سے اس تفصیل کی وجہ سے جو گذر چکی ہے۔ تشریح. مصنف فرماتے ہیں کہ یہ تفصیل مذکور اس وقت ہے جبکہ منبر فقہ وہ اور اگر وقتہ نہ ہولیکن عمر کا غالب گمان یہ ہے کہ زید سچا ہے اب بھی اس کا قول مقبول ہوگا کیونکہ یہ گذر چکا ہے کہ معاملات میں مخبر کا عادل ہونا لازم اور شریا نہیں ہے۔ پس فرق یہ ہوا کہ ثقہ ہونے کی صورت میں براہ راست اس کا قول معتبر ہے اور غیر ثقہ ہونے کی صورت میں بعد تحری جبکہ اس کے صدق کا غالب گمان ہو ۔
غالب گمان اس کے جھوٹے ہونے کا ہے تو قول معتبر نہیں ہوگا
وان كان اكبر رائه الكاذب لم يسع له ان يتعرض بشئي من ذلك لان اكبر الراى يقام مقام اليقين
ترجمہ .... اور اگر اس کا غالب گمان یہ ہو کہ یہ جھوٹا ہے تو اس کیلئے گنجائش نہیں ہے کہ اہں، میں سے کسی شی ء سے تعرض کرے اسلئے کہ گمان غالب یقین کے قائم مقام ہوتا ہے۔
In transactions, the statement of one person is reliable, and his being sane is sufficient, whether male or female, Muslim or disbeliever, just or sinful. One issue derived from this principle: He said (Muhammad): Whoever knows that a slave girl belongs to a man, then sees another selling her and says, 'The owner appointed me as agent to sell her,' then it is permissible for him to buy her and have intercourse with her, because he gave a correct report with no one disputing it. And the statement of one person in transactions is accepted regardless of his description, due to what has passed. Similarly, if he says, 'I bought her from him,' or 'He gifted her to me,' or 'He gave her to me as charity,' for the same reason we mentioned. Translation: Muhammad said in al-Jami' al-Saghir: Whoever knows that a slave girl belongs to a certain person, then sees another selling her and he says, 'The owner appointed me as agent to sell her,' then he has leeway to buy her and have intercourse with her because he gave a correct report with no one disputing it. The statement of one person in transactions is accepted regardless of his description, due to the proof that passed earlier. Similarly, if he says, 'I bought her from him,' or 'He gifted her to me,' or 'He gave her to me as charity,' due to the proof we mentioned. Commentary: It has passed earlier that in transactions, the statement of one person is accepted, and only sanity is sufficient, whether male or female, Muslim or disbeliever, just or sinful. So when Zaid is seen selling a slave girl in the market, and Umar knows that the girl belongs to Khalid, but Zaid says that Khalid appointed him as agent to sell, or says that he bought her from Khalid, or Khalid gifted her to him, or gave her as charity, then in all these cases Umar is permitted to trust Zaid's statement and buy the girl and have intercourse with her, because the informant has intellect and religion, and lying is forbidden for him, so the report is correct and there is no opponent. And this is a transaction where one person's statement is accepted. If the informant is not trustworthy (ghayr thiqah) but his truthfulness is his dominant presumption, then his statement is considered reliable. And this (accepting his statement) is when he is trustworthy. Similarly, if he is not trustworthy but his dominant presumption is that he is truthful, because the justice of the informant in transactions is not required due to need, as previously mentioned. Commentary: The author says that this detail applies when the informant is trustworthy. If he is not trustworthy but Umar's dominant presumption is that Zaid is truthful, then his statement is still acceptable, because it has passed that in transactions the informant's justice is not a legal requirement. Thus the difference is: in the case of trustworthiness, his statement is directly reliable; in the case of non-trustworthiness, after investigation, when his truthfulness becomes the dominant presumption. If his dominant presumption is that he is lying, then he is not permitted to involve himself in any of that. Translation: ... and if his dominant presumption is that he is lying, then he has no leeway to involve himself in any of that, because dominant presumption stands in place of certainty.
Source pages p. 367

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement with expansion (sharh) on the principle that in transactions (mu'amalat), the unconfirmed statement of a single person is accepted as sufficient evidence for action, provided that the hearer has a dominant presumption (ghalabat al-zann) of its truthfulness. The principle is applied to a specific case: a man sees a slave girl being sold by someone who claims to be the owner's agent. If the potential buyer has a strong belief that the seller is truthful, he may purchase the slave and even have intercourse with her, based on that statement. The passage distinguishes between a trustworthy (thiqah) informant, whose word is accepted directly, and a non-trustworthy informant, whose word is only accepted if after assessment the hearer's dominant presumption leans toward truthfulness. If the dominant presumption is that the informant is lying, then it is not permissible to act on his statement. This is because in transactions, need (hajah) relaxes the condition of the informant's 'adalah (justice), unlike in formal testimony (shahadah). The commentary emphasizes that sanity is the only condition; gender, religion, and justice are irrelevant in this context.

Key Terms
ثقہ (thiqah): A trustworthy person whose word is reliable without further investigation.
غالب گمان (ghālib al-zann): Dominant presumption; the stronger belief in the mind, which in Hanafi law is given the weight of certainty in worldly matters.
مخبر (mukhbi): Informant; the person who gives a report or statement in a transaction.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Only if the seller is a just witness
B) Only if the buyer has a dominant presumption that the seller is truthful
C) Only if the real owner confirms
D) Only if the slave girl consents

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the statement of one person in transactions is accepted if the hearer has a dominant presumption of truthfulness, based on practical need.

Ruling When Informant Is Unreliable
Ruling on buyer's reliance on predominant opinion when informant is not trustworthy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of predominant opinion (zann ghalib) in transactions where the informant's trustworthiness is uncertain, and state the legal consequence when predominant opinion indicates falsehood.

Core Idea
When an unreliable informant speaks, predominant opinion (zann ghalib) replaces certainty in legal rulings.

This lesson examines a scenario from Hanafi sales law: a buyer is approached by a person claiming to sell a slave girl. The buyer does not know the true owner. If the informant is trustworthy, his word is accepted; if not, the buyer must seek predominant opinion (taharrī) about the informant's truthfulness. The Hanafi ruling introduces the key principle that predominant opinion (zann ghalib) stands in place of certainty when certainty is unavailable.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and scenario
The buyer is approached by a possessor (صاحب اليد) who claims to be an agent selling a slave girl on behalf of the true owner. The buyer does not know the owner.
2
Distinguish between a trustworthy and untrustworthy informant
If the informant is trustworthy (ثقة), his statement is accepted. If he is not trustworthy, the buyer must perform taharrī (seek predominant opinion) to decide.
Trustworthiness is judged by the buyer's knowledge or external factors.
3
Analyze the reasoning behind the distinction
The informant's statement about himself (that he is not the owner) is credible. But his statement about the owner's authorization is not automatically proof; it only provides predominant opinion. Hence, the buyer must rely on his own predominant opinion after investigation.
The text states: لان اخباره حجة في حقه (his informing is a proof with respect to himself).
4
Apply the principle of zann ghalib
In Hanafi usul, predominant opinion (ظن غالب) is equivalent to certainty in legal rulings when certainty is absent. If the buyer's predominant opinion is that the informant is lying, it is not permissible to buy and have intercourse.
This shows the weight Hanafi school gives to strong probability.
5
State the practical ruling
If the informant is not trustworthy and the buyer's predominant opinion indicates falsehood, the transaction is impermissible (makruh tahrimi). If the predominant opinion indicates truthfulness, it may be permissible.
The ruling is specific to the buyer's internal state; external proof would override.

Source Text

تشریح اگر مخبر عادل ثقتہ نہیں ہے تو پھر مشتری کیلئے تحری کا حکم ہے تحری کے بعد اس کا غالب گمان یہ ہوا کہ یہ جھوٹ بول رہا ہے تو چونکہ ظن غالب یقین کے قائم مقام ہوتا ہے۔ اسلئے اس کیلئے یہ جائز نہیں ہے کہ وہ اس باندی کوخریدےاوراس سے وطی کرے۔ مشتری کو معلوم نہیں تھا کہ یہ کس کی باندی ہے قبضہ والے نے بتایا کہ فلاں کی باندی ہے اس نے مجھے بیچنے کا وکیل بنایا ہے
وكذا اذالم يعلم انها الفلان ولكن اخبره صاحب اليدانها لفلان وانه وكله ببيعها او اشتراها منه والمخبر ثقة قبل قــولـــــه وان لم يكن ثقة يعتبر اكير الراى لان اخیا لاره حجة في حقه ترجمہ اور ایسے ہی جب کی وہ نہیں جانتا کہ یہ باندی فلاں کی ہے لیکن قبضہ والے نے اس کو خبر دی کہ یہ فلاں کی ہے اور اس نے اس کو (یعنی مجھے ) اس کو بیچنے کو وکیل بنانا ہے یاس نے (میں نے ) اس کو اس سے خریدا ہے اور منجر ثقہ ہے تو اس کا قول قبول کر لیا جائے گا اور اگر ثقہ نہ ہو تو گمان غالب کا اعتبار کیا جائے گا اس لئے کہ اس کا خبر دینا اپنے حق میں حجت ہے۔ تشریح مشتری کو کچھ معلوم نہیں کہ باندی کسن کی ہے قبضہ والے نے بتایا کہ یہ خالد کی ہے اور اس نے مجھے اسکے بیچنے کا وکیل بنایا ہے یا میں نے اس کو خالد سے خریدا ہے تو اگر مخبر ثقہ ہے۔ تو اس کا قول قبول کر لیا جائے گا اور اگر ثقہ نہ ہو تو حسب سابق گمان غالب کا اعتبار ہوگا۔ سوال آپ نے یہاں ظن غالب کا اعتبار کیوں کیا ؟
جواب مخبر نے اپنے بارے میں جو خبر دی کہ میں اس کا مالک نہیں بلکہ اس کا مالک خالد ہے، اس کی یہ خبر تو معتبر ہے لیکن خالد کے بارے میں ہو اس نے یہ خبر دی کہ خالد نے مجھے اس کے فروخت کرنے کا وکیل بنایا ہے یہ خبر حجت نہیں ہے اس لئے کہ ہم نے ظن غالب اعتبار کیا ہے اسی کو مصنف نے فرمایا کہ اس کی خبر اپنے حق میں حجت ہے۔
Explanation: If the informant is not a just and trustworthy person, then the buyer must seek [تحری]. After seeking, if his predominant opinion is that he is lying, then because predominant opinion (ظن غالب) stands in place of certainty, it is not permissible for him to buy this slave girl and have intercourse with her. The buyer did not know whose slave girl she is; the possessor informed that she belongs to so-and-so and that he appointed me as agent to sell her. Also: If he does not know that she belongs to so-and-so, but the possessor informed him that she belongs to so-and-so and that he appointed him to sell her, or he bought her from him, and the informant is trustworthy, his statement is accepted. If he is not trustworthy, the predominant opinion is considered, because his informing is a proof with respect to himself. Translation and explanation: Similarly... Question: Why did you consider predominant opinion here? Answer: The informant's information about himself—that he is not the owner but the owner is Khalid—is credible, but his information about Khalid—that Khalid appointed him to sell—is not a proof; thus we considered predominant opinion. The author said that his information is a proof with respect to himself.
Source pages p. 369

Commentary
This is a ruling statement followed by explanation. The passage addresses a situation where a buyer is approached by a person (possessor) claiming to sell a slave girl. The buyer does not know the true owner. If the informant is trustworthy (ثقة), his word is accepted. If he is not trustworthy, the buyer must exercise تحری (seeking predominant opinion) to decide whether to proceed. The reasoning is that the informant's statement about himself (that he is not the owner) is credible, but his claim about the owner's authorization is not automatically credible; thus predominant opinion (ظن غالب) is considered, as it stands in place of certainty in legal matters. This is a Hanafi principle where ظن غالب is given weight in the absence of certainty.

Key Terms
ظن غالب (zann ghalib): Predominant opinion or strong probability that stands in place of certainty in legal rulings when certainty is unavailable.
تحري (taharrī): The process of seeking the predominant opinion through reflection and investigation when facing conflicting information or uncertainty.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Ask for witnesses
C) Seek predominant opinion (تحري)
D) Accept the statement if the informant is a Muslim

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi sales law, when an informant is not trustworthy, the buyer must rely on predominant opinion (zann ghalib) which stands in place of certainty to determine permissibility.

Buying from a Silent Possessor: Knowledge of Original Ownership
Rulings when possessor gives no info

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi rulings regarding buying from a possessor who gives no information and identify the key factor (buyer's knowledge of original owner) that changes the ruling from prohibition to permissibility.

Core Idea
Possession is strong evidence of ownership; but if you know the original owner, you must verify the transfer before buying.

This passage from al-Hidayah deals with a common practical scenario: someone offers an item for sale but does not explain how they got it. The Hanafi school distinguishes two situations: when the buyer knows the original owner, and when he does not. In the first case, the buyer must delay the purchase until the chain of ownership is clarified; in the second, the purchase is permissible even if the seller is of bad character. The reasoning turns on the evidentiary weight of possession (yad al-mutasarrif) as a sign of ownership.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage presents two distinct cases based on whether the buyer knows the original owner or not. Locate the two conditional phrases: 'if he knew it as belonging to the first owner' and 'if he does not know that.'
The ruling changes dramatically between these two scenarios.
2
Apply the first ruling: buyer knows original owner
If the buyer recognizes the item as originally belonging to someone else (e.g., Khalid), he must not buy it until he learns how it transferred to the current possessor. The original owner's prior possession is strong evidence of ownership, and a valid cause (sabab) for transfer is needed.
This is a prohibition (not permissible) until the transfer is known.
3
Apply the second ruling: buyer does not know original owner
If the buyer has no knowledge of a prior owner, he may buy the item even if the possessor is sinful (fasiq). The hand (possession) of the current holder is taken as evidence of ownership, overriding any moral concerns.
Permissibility holds despite the seller's character due to the principle that possession is a valid indicator.
4
Understand the proof: possession as evidence
The proof for both rulings is the same: 'the hand of the person in control is evidence of ownership' (yad al-mutasarrif daleel al-milk). This is a qiyas based on the general principle that external possession reflects ownership unless contradictory evidence appears.
The Hanafi position gives possession strong evidentiary weight, unlike some schools that may require the seller to be trustworthy.
5
Note the strength of possession even from a sinful person
The text explicitly states that the hand of a sinful person is as much evidence as that of a just person, and no opponent (mu'arid) is present to contradict it. The apparent proof (dalil zahir) takes precedence over mere predominant opinion (ghalabat al-ghann).
This underscores Hanafi reliance on external, objective indicators over subjective assumptions.

Source Text

مشتری کو علم ہو تو بائع اس وقت تک نہ خریدے جیہی ہے اس کے پاس آنے کی وجہ معلوم نہ ہو جائے
وان لم يخبره صاحب اليد بشئى فان كان عرفها للاول لم يشترها حتى يعلم انتقالها الى ملك الثاني لان يد الأول دليل ملكه وان كان لا يعرف ذلك له ان يشتريها وان كان ذوا اليد فاسقا لان يد الفاسق دليل الملك في حق الفاسق والعدل ولم يعارضه معارض ولا معتبر با كيرالراى عند وجود الدليل الظاهر
ترجمہ اور اگر اس کو قبضہ والے نے کچھ خبر نہ دی پس اگر وہ باندی کو پہچانتا ہے اول کے لئے تو اس کو نہ خریدے یہاں تک کہ وہ دوسرے کی ملک کی جانب اس کے انتقال کو جان لے اس لئے کہ اول کا قبضہ اس کی ملک کی دلیل ہے اور اگر وہ اس کو نہ پہچا نتا ہو تو اس کے لئے جائز ہے کہ اس کو خریدے اگر چہ قبضہ والا فاسق ہو اس لئے کہ فاسق کا قبضہ ملک کی دلیل ہے فاسق اور عادل کے حق میں اور اس کا کوئی معارض نہیں اور دلیل ظاہر کے وجود کے وقت گمان غالب کا اعتبار نہیں کیا جاتا ہے۔
تشریح بائع نے کوئی خبر نہیں دی مگر مشتری جانتا ہے کہ یہ باندی خالد کی ہے تو جب تک مشتری کے سامنے یہ بات واضح نہ ہو جائے
كہ خالد کے پاس سے بائع کے پاس یہ باندی کیسے آئی تو اس کو نہ خریدے کیوں کہ اس پر جب پہلے خالد کا قبضہ تھا تو یہ اس بات کی دلیل تھی کہ خالد ہی اس کا مالک ہے تو دوسرے کی ملک میں آنے کے لئے کوئی سبب چاہیے۔ اور اگر مشتری بھی نہیں جانتا اور نہ بائع نے خبر دی تو مشتری اس کو خرید سکتا ہے اگر چہ بائع ( ذوالید ) فاسق بھی ہو اس لئے کہ متصرف کا قبضہ اس بات کی دلیل ہے کہ یہی اس کا مالک ہے خواہ وہ فاسق ہو یا عادل، اور نہ کوئی اس کا معارض ہے۔
If the buyer knows, the seller should not buy until the reason for its coming to him becomes known. And if the possessor does not inform him of anything: if he knew it as belonging to the first owner, he should not buy it until he knows its transfer to the ownership of the second, because the hand of the first is evidence of his ownership. And if he does not know that, he may buy it, even if the possessor is sinful, because the hand of the sinful is evidence of ownership for the sinful and the just, and there is no opponent to it, and the predominant opinion is not considered when there is an apparent proof. Translation: ... Explanation: The seller gave no news, but the buyer knows that this slave girl is Khalid's. So until it becomes clear to the buyer how this slave girl came from Khalid to the seller, he should not buy, because Khalid's previous possession is evidence that he is the owner, and a reason is needed for transfer to another's ownership. And if the buyer also does not know, and the seller gave no news, the buyer may buy it, even if the seller (possessor) is sinful, because the possession of the person in control is evidence that he is the owner, whether he is sinful or just, and there is no opponent.
Source pages p. 369

Commentary
This passage presents two sub-rulings. First: If the buyer knows the original owner (e.g., Khalid) but the current possessor gives no information, the buyer must not buy until he learns how the slave girl transferred to the possessor. This is because the original owner's possession is strong evidence of ownership, and a change requires a valid cause. Second: If the buyer does not know the original owner and the possessor does not inform, the buyer may buy even if the possessor is sinful (فاسق). The reasoning is that possession itself is evidence of ownership regardless of the possessor's moral character, and since there is no contradictory evidence, the apparent proof (دليل ظاهر) of possession prevails, overriding any need for predominant opinion (ظن غالب).

Key Terms
يد المتصرف (yad al-mutasarrif): Possession of the person who is in control of the item, considered evidence of ownership in Hanafi law.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If the buyer knows that the slave girl originally belonged to Khalid, and the current possessor gives no information, what must the buyer do?
A) He may buy immediately because possession is evidence
B) He must not buy until he knows how it transferred
C) He must seek predominant opinion
D) He can only buy if the possessor is trustworthy

Key Takeaway
A buyer's knowledge of a prior owner triggers an obligation to verify the chain of transfer; otherwise, the current possessor's hand is sufficient proof of ownership.

Abstention When Possession Is Unlikely
Ruling on recommended abstention when item is unlikely to be owned by the possessor

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that possession is evidence of ownership, identify the exception when possession is improbable, and state the recommended abstention.

Core Idea
When the possessor is unlikely to own the item, abstaining is recommended, but the purchase remains valid due to possession as legal proof.

This passage discusses a critical nuance in Hanafi fiqh: although possession (يد) is generally accepted as evidence of ownership, there is an exception when the item is of a kind that the possessor would not normally own. In such cases, the buyer is recommended to abstain out of caution, but the sale is still valid if he proceeds, because he relied on the apparent legal proof. This lesson unpacks that balance between scrupulousness and legal validity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
The passage begins by affirming that possession is evidence of ownership, whether the possessor is sinful or just. This is the default Hanafi ruling.
Allama Ayni corrects the text to say 'hand of the possessor' to avoid confusion.
2
Recognize the exception
The exception occurs when the item is such that its like cannot be owned by the possessor (e.g., a poor person selling an expensive cloth). In that case, possession alone is still evidence, but it becomes suspicious.
Example: a poor person selling a luxury item or an illiterate selling a rare book.
3
Determine the recommended action
In such suspicious circumstances, it is recommended (مستحب) to abstain from buying, out of scrupulousness (تنزه) and caution.
This is not obligatory; it is a recommended piety.
4
Understand the legal outcome if he buys
If the buyer proceeds despite the suspicion, the purchase is still valid and he is free from blame because he relied on the legal proof (possession).
The phrase 'يرجى ان يكون في سعة من ذلك' indicates that he is hoped to be at ease.
5
Note the textual correction by Allama Ayni
The passage begins with a note that the wording should say 'hand of the possessor' rather than 'hand of the sinful', since possession is evidence regardless of character.
This ensures the ruling is understood as general and not limited to sinful possessors.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تنبيه لان يد الفاسق الخ ... بظاہر عبارت سے معلوم ہوتا ہے کہ جب فاسق کا قبضہ ہے تو یہ ملک کی دلیل ہے خواہ خریدنے والا عادل ہو یا فاسق لیکن یہاں مقام کا تقاضہ یہ تھا کہ قبضہ خواہ فاسق کا ہو یا عادل کا ، یہ ملک کی دلیل ہے اس لئے علامہ عینی نے کہا ہے کہ عبارت یوں ہونی چاہیے تھی لان يد المتصرف دليل الملك في حق الفاسق والعادل
میع ایسی چیز ہے کہ اس کے پاس ہونا دشوار ہے تو مستحب نہ ، خرید نا ہے
الا ان يكون مثله لا يملك مثل ذلك فيحننا، يستحب له ان يتنزه ومع ذلك لو اشتراها يرجى ان يكون في سعة من ذلك لاعتماده الدليل الشرعي
ترجمه مگر یہ کہ اس کا مثل اس کے مثل کا مالک نہ ہو سکے تو اس وقت اس کے لئے مستحب ہے کہ اس سے پر ہیز کرے اور اس کے
با وجود اس کو خرید لیا تو امید کی جاتی ہے کہ وہ اس کی گنجائش میں ہے اس کے اعتماد کرنے کی وجہ سے دلیل شرعی پر۔ تشریح۔ ہاں اگر صبح ایسا ہو کہ ایسا میچ ہونا اس لئے کہ دشوار ہو مثل قیمتی ہوتی کوئی فقیر بیچتا ہے یا کوئی جاہل ابن جاہل کوئی عربی کی کتاب فروخت کرتا ہے تو یہ ظاہر آ یہ چوری کی دلیل ہے تو ایسے وقت مستحب یہ ہے کہ نہ خریدے کیونکہ تنزہ اور احتیاط اسی میں ہے البتہ اس کے با وجود اگر اس نے خرید لیا تو اس نے دلیل شرعی پر اعتماد کیا ہے اور دلیل شرعی قبضہ ہے۔
Note: Because the hand of the sinful... etc. From the apparent wording, it seems that when the sinful has possession, it is evidence of ownership whether the buyer is just or sinful. But the context required that possession, whether by sinful or just, is evidence of ownership. Hence Allama Ayni said that the wording should have been: because the hand of the possessor is evidence of ownership for the sinful and the just. The exception is if the item is such that its like cannot own the like; then it is recommended for him to abstain, yet despite that if he buys it, it is hoped that he will be at ease because he relied on the legal proof. Translation: Except that when the like of him cannot own the like of that, then it is recommended for him to abstain. And despite that if he buys it, it is hoped that he is at ease from that, due to his reliance on the legal proof. Explanation: Yes, if it is such that having such an item is difficult, e.g., an expensive cloth being sold by a poor person, or an Arabic book sold by an ignorant person, then this is apparently evidence of theft. So at such time it is recommended not to buy, because scrupulousness and caution lie in that. However, despite that if he bought it, he relied on the legal proof, and the legal proof is possession.
Source pages p. 370

Commentary
This passage begins with a note (تنبيه) correcting the earlier wording. The commentator (Allama Ayni) suggests that the text should say 'hand of the possessor' rather than 'hand of the sinful', because possession is evidence regardless of character. Then it presents an exception: if the item is of a kind that the possessor would not normally own (e.g., a poor person selling an expensive cloth, an illiterate selling a rare Arabic book), then it is recommended (مستحب) to abstain from buying, because the situation creates strong suspicion of stolen goods. However, if the buyer still purchases, it is valid and permissible because he relied on the legal evidence (possession). This illustrates the Hanafi balance between caution and reliance on apparent legal proofs.

Key Terms
تنزه (tanaẓẓuh): Abstaining from a permissible act out of caution and piety to avoid potential sin.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is haram to buy
B) It is recommended to abstain, but buying is valid
C) The buyer must investigate further
D) The sale is invalid because of suspicion

Key Takeaway
Possession is legal evidence of ownership; however, when the item is improbable for the possessor, abstention is recommended but the purchase remains valid.

Buying from a Slave: Inquiry is Required
Ruling with rational proof on buying from a slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a slave brings a slave girl for sale, explain why inquiry is required, and apply the conditions of trustworthiness and predominant opinion.

Core Idea
A slave cannot own property, so buying without inquiry is prohibited; evidence of master's permission is required.

When a slave or maidservant brings a slave girl for sale, the buyer cannot simply assume the slave has authority to sell. In Hanafi law, a slave does not have independent ownership; all property in a slave's possession belongs to the master. Therefore, the buyer must actively inquire about the master's permission before proceeding. This lesson unpacks the ruling and the rational proof behind it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is buying a slave girl brought by a slave. The ruling: it is not permissible without inquiry; the buyer must first ask.
2
Understand the reason
Because the slave has no ownership (la malk lahu). Thus ownership belongs to the master, so the slave cannot sell unless authorized.
This is the core proof (dalil) in the passage.
3
Apply the conditions of acceptance
If the slave states his master permitted him and he is trustworthy (thiqah), it is accepted. If not trustworthy, the buyer follows predominant opinion (akbar al-ra'y). If still uncertain, do not buy.
The text outlines three scenarios.
4
Recognize the impediment (hajiz)
The lack of ownership creates an impediment to the transaction. Evidence (dalil) is necessary to remove it; hence inquiry is mandated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
غلام یا باندی باندی کو فروخت کرنے کیلئے لائے تو تحقیق کے بغیر تصرف نہ کرے
وان كان الذى اناه بها عبدا وامة لم يقبلها ولم يشترها حتى يسأل لان المملوك لا ملك له فيعلم ان الملك فيها لغيره فان اخبره ان مولاه اذن له وهو ثقة قبل وان لم يكن ثقة يعتبر اكبر الراى وان لم يكن له راى لم يشترها لقيام الحاجز فلا بد من دليل.
ترجمہ اور اگر وہ جو اس کے پاس باندی کو لایا ہے غلام یا با ندھی تو اس کو قبول نہ کرے اور نہ اس کو خریدے یہاں تک کہ سوال کرے اسلئے کہ مملوک اس کیلئے کوئی ملک نہیں ہے تو یہ بات معلوم ہوئی کہ ملک اس میں اس کے غیر کی ہے پس اس نے اس کو خبر دی کہ اس کے
2.
If a slave or maidservant brings a slave girl for sale, he should not take possession nor buy until he asks, because the slave has no ownership, so it is known that ownership belongs to someone else. Then if he informs him that his master permitted him and he is trustworthy, it is accepted. If he is not trustworthy, the predominant opinion is considered. And if he has no opinion, he should not buy, because an impediment exists, so evidence is necessary. Translation: And if the one who brings the slave girl to him is a slave or maidservant, then he should not accept her nor buy her until he asks, because the slave has no ownership, so it is known that ownership belongs to someone else. Then if he informs him that his master permitted him and he is trustworthy, it is accepted. If he is not trustworthy, the predominant opinion is considered. And if he has no opinion, he should not buy, because the impediment exists, so there must be evidence.
Source pages p. 370

Commentary
This passage addresses a situation where a slave (or maidservant) brings a slave girl for sale. Since a slave cannot own property (in Hanafi law, a slave does not have independent ownership), it is evident that the slave girl belongs to someone else—the master. Therefore, the buyer must not immediately accept or buy; he must inquire. If the slave states that his master gave him permission and the slave is trustworthy, it is accepted. If not trustworthy, the buyer uses predominant opinion (ظن غالب). If he has no opinion either way, he must not buy, because the lack of ownership creates an impediment (حاجز) to the transaction without proper evidence. This ruling protects against unlawful transactions involving property that may not be authorized by the true owner.

Key Terms
مملوك (mamluk): A slave; in Hanafi law, a slave does not have legal capacity to own property independently.
ثقة (thiqah): Trustworthy; a person whose reliability is established.
أكبر الرأي (akbar al-ra'y): Predominant opinion; a strong probability formed after considering available evidence.
حاجز (hajiz): Impediment; a barrier that prevents a transaction from being valid unless removed by evidence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What must the buyer do when a slave brings a slave girl for sale?
A) Buy immediately if the price is fair
B) Ask the slave about the master's permission
C) Assume the slave is the owner
D) Reject the offer outright

Key Takeaway
A slave cannot legally own property, so a buyer must verify the master's permission before accepting a sale from a slave.

Buying from a Possessor: Yad as Evidence of Ownership
Ruling on buying from a possessor and exception for improbable ownership

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on buying from a possessor, explain why possession is considered decisive evidence, and identify the exceptional case where avoiding the purchase is recommended.

Core Idea
Possession (yad) is a definitive proof of ownership, making a sale valid even without further inquiry, unless the possession is improbable.

In Hanafi fiqh, possession (yad) is a primary indicator of ownership. This principle simplifies transactions and reduces the burden of proof on buyers. However, when possession is clearly improbable, the school recommends caution. This lesson examines the ruling and its exception as presented in Ashraf al-Hidayah.

Guided Path
1
State the general ruling
Identify that buying from a possessor is permissible (mubah) because possession is evidence of ownership, even if the seller is a fasiq (sinner).
The ruling applies to both just and sinful sellers.
2
Understand the proof
Recognize why possession is considered a definitive proof (dalil qati) that overrides overwhelming presumption (ghalib al-zann). The Shariah has made yad a legal indicator.
Ghalib al-zann is irrelevant when a definitive proof exists.
3
Analyze the exception
Note that when possession is highly improbable (e.g., a poor person selling a very valuable item), it becomes mustahab (recommended) to avoid the purchase, but the sale remains valid if conducted.
The exception is based on scrupulousness (tanzih), not prohibition.
4
Reflect on the tanbih
Understand Allamah Ayni's correction: the phrase 'hand of the fasiq' should read 'hand of the possessor', emphasizing that the evidence is from possession itself, not the seller's character.
This refinement ensures the ruling is correctly applied.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
کہ خالد کے پاس سے بائع کے پاس یہ باندی کیسے آئی تو اس کو نہ خریدے کیوں کہ اس پر جب پہلے خالد کا قبضہ تھا تو یہ اس بات کی دلیل تھی کہ خالد ہی اس کا مالک ہے تو دوسرے کی ملک میں آنے کے لئے کوئی سبب چاہیے۔ اور اگر مشتری بھی نہیں جانتا اور نہ بائع نے خبر دی تو مشتری اس کو خرید سکتا ہے اگر چہ بائع ( ذوالید ) فاسق بھی ہو اس لئے کہ متصرف کا قبضہ اس بات کی دلیل ہے کہ یہی اس کا مالک ہے خواہ وہ فاسق ہو یا عادل، اور نہ کوئی اس کا معارض ہے۔
سوال گمان غالب کا یہاں کیوں انتہاز میں کیا گیا ہے؟ جواب .. اس سے قومی دلیل جس کو دلیل ظاہر کہا گیا ہے موجود ہے۔ یعنی قبضہ دلیل ملک موجود ہے تو گمان غالب پھر کوئی اعتبار نہ ہوگا۔
تنبيه لان يد الفاسق الخ ... بظاہر عبارت سے معلوم ہوتا ہے کہ جب فاسق کا قبضہ ہے تو یہ ملک کی دلیل ہے خواہ خریدنے والا عادل ہو یا فاسق لیکن یہاں مقام کا تقاضہ یہ تھا کہ قبضہ خواہ فاسق کا ہو یا عادل کا ، یہ ملک کی دلیل ہے اس لئے علامہ عینی نے کہا ہے کہ عبارت یوں ہونی چاہیے تھی لان يد المتصرف دليل الملك في حق الفاسق والعادل
مثل ایسی چیز ہے کہ اس کے پاس ہونا دشوار ہے تو مستحب نہ خریدنا ہے
الا ان يكون مثله لا يملك مثل ذلك فيحننا، يستحب له ان يتنزه ومع ذلك لو اشتراها يرجى ان يكون في سعة من ذلك لاعتماده الدليل الشرعي
ترجمه مگر یہ کہ اس کا مثل اس کے مثل کا مالک نہ ہو سکے تو اس وقت اس کے لئے مستحب ہے کہ اس سے پر ہیز کرے اور اس کے با وجود اس کو خرید لیا تو امید کی جاتی ہے کہ وہ اس کی گنجائش میں ہے اس کے اعتماد کرنے کی وجہ سے دلیل شرعی پر۔ تشریح۔ ہاں اگر صبح ایسا ہو کہ ایسا مثل ہونا اس لئے کہ دشوار ہو مثل قیمتی ہوتی کوئی فقیر بیچتا ہے یا کوئی جاہل ابن جاہل کوئی عربی کی کتاب فروخت کرتا ہے تو یہ ظاہر آ یہ چوری کی دلیل ہے تو ایسے وقت مستحب یہ ہے کہ نہ خریدے کیونکہ تنزہ اور احتیاط اسی میں ہے البتہ اس کے با وجود اگر اس نے خرید لیا تو اس نے دلیل شرعی پر اعتماد کیا ہے اور دلیل شرعی قبضہ ہے۔
If a slave-girl comes to the seller from Khalid's possession, he should not buy her because when Khalid had possession of her, that was evidence that Khalid is her owner, so some reason is needed for her to come into another's ownership. And if the buyer does not know and the seller did not inform, the buyer may buy her even if the seller (the possessor) is a fasiq, because the possessor's possession is evidence that he is the owner, whether he is fasiq or just, and there is no one opposing him. Question: Why is overwhelming presumption (ghalib al-zann) not considered here? Answer: A definitive proof, called the apparent proof, is present. That is, possession as evidence of ownership is present, so overwhelming presumption has no weight. Warning: The phrase 'because the hand of the fasiq...' apparently indicates that when a fasiq has possession, it is evidence of ownership whether the buyer is just or fasiq. But the context required that possession, whether of a fasiq or just person, is evidence of ownership. Therefore, Allamah Ayni said the phrase should be: 'because the hand of the possessor is evidence of ownership for the fasiq and the just.' This refers to an item whose possession is difficult for him; then it is mustahab not to buy. Except if someone like him cannot possess such an item, then it is mustahab for him to avoid, and even so if he buys it, it is hoped that he will be in a position of leniency because he relied on the legal proof. Translation: But if his like cannot own the likes of that, then at that time it is mustahab for him to avoid, and despite that if he bought it, it is hoped that he is in a position of leniency due to his reliance on the legal proof. Explanation: Yes, if it is such that such an item is difficult for him, like a poor person selling a valuable horse, or an ignorant person selling an Arabic book, then this clearly indicates theft, so at such a time it is mustahab not to buy because scrupulousness and caution are in this. However, if despite that he bought it, he has relied on the legal proof, and the legal proof is possession.
Source pages p. 370

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf al-Hidaya presents a ruling on the validity of buying from a person who has possession (yad) of an item. The primary principle is that possession is a valid proof of ownership in Islamic law. Therefore, if a buyer does not know the history of how the item came to the seller, he may purchase it based solely on the seller's possession, even if the seller is known to be a fasiq (sinner). The passage addresses a potential objection: why is ghalib al-zann (overwhelming presumption) not considered to override the apparent proof? The answer is that possession itself is a definitive proof (dalil qati) in the absence of any opposing evidence, so ghalib al-zann has no role. A warning (tanbih) is given regarding the wording of the original text: the phrase 'the hand of the fasiq' might suggest that only the fasiq's possession is evidence, but the correct wording should be 'the hand of the possessor' to include both fasiq and just persons, as stated by Allamah Ayni. The passage then discusses an exception: if the item is such that it is highly improbable for the seller to own it (e.g., a poor person selling a very valuable item), then it becomes mustahab (recommended) to avoid buying, because the improbability indicates theft. However, if the buyer proceeds with the purchase, it is still valid because he relied on the legal proof (possession).

Key Terms
ید (yad): Possession or physical control over an item, considered a proof of ownership in Hanafi law.
ظن غالب (ghalib al-zann): Overwhelming presumption or preponderant belief, which is given weight in the absence of definitive proof.
متصرف (mutasarif): The person who has possession and is acting (selling) the item.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling in this passage, if a buyer purchases an item from a seller who is a fasiq (sinner) and the buyer has no knowledge of how the seller acquired it, what is the status of the sale?
A) The sale is invalid because the fasiq is not trustworthy.
B) The sale is valid because possession is evidence of ownership.
C) The sale is valid only if the buyer performs investigation (taharri).
D) The sale is makruh and should not be done.

Key Takeaway
Possession (yad) is a definitive proof of ownership in Hanafi law, making it permissible to buy from a possessor unless the possession is highly improbable, in which case avoidance is recommended but the sale remains valid.

Buying from a Slave: Conditions for Valid Transaction
Ruling with stepwise conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a buyer may purchase a slave-girl brought by a slave, and identify when the buyer must refrain from the transaction.

Core Idea
A slave cannot own property, so verify permission: accept only if trustworthy or overwhelming presumption exists.

This passage addresses a specific scenario in sales: a slave brings a slave-girl for sale. Since a slave lacks independent ownership, the buyer cannot proceed blindly. The Hanafi school outlines a graduated approach: investigate, rely on trustworthiness, then overwhelming presumption, and if still doubtful, refrain. This protects against invalid transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal incapacity
Recognize that a slave (mamluk) does not have ownership capacity in Hanafi law. Therefore, any property brought by a slave presumptively belongs to the master.
This is a foundational principle: al-mamluk la milk lahu.
2
Determine the initial ruling
The buyer must not accept or purchase the slave-girl without inquiry. This is a prohibition (haram) until investigation is done.
The text says: 'he should not transact without investigation'.
3
Evaluate the slave's claim of permission
If the slave informs the buyer that the master gave permission, the buyer must assess the slave's trustworthiness (thiqah). If the slave is thiqah, his statement is accepted and the transaction is valid.
Thiqah means a person whose word is reliable in legal matters.
4
Apply the fallback: overwhelming presumption
If the slave is not thiqah, the buyer must rely on his own overwhelming presumption (ghalib al-zann) based on circumstantial evidence. This could include the slave's demeanor, the normal practice of the master, etc.
Ghalib al-zann is a valid basis in Hanafi muamalat.
5
Conclude when doubt remains
If the buyer has no formed opinion (ra'y) and remains in doubt, he must not buy. The obstacle (hajiz) of the slave's lack of ownership requires a positive proof (dalil) to overcome.
The proof is either the trustworthy statement or overwhelming presumption.

Source Text

وان كان الذى اناه بها عبدا وامة لم يقبلها ولم يشترها حتى يسأل لان المملوك لا ملك له فيعلم ان الملك فيها لغيره فان اخبره ان مولاه اذن له وهو ثقة قبل وان لم يكن ثقة يعتبر اكبر الراى وان لم يكن له راى لم يشترها لقيام الحاجز فلا بد من دليل.

If a slave or slave-girl brings a slave-girl for sale, he should not transact without investigation. If the one who brought her is a male slave or female slave, he should not accept her nor buy her until he asks, because the slave has no ownership, so it is known that the ownership belongs to someone else. If he informs him that his master allowed him and he is trustworthy (thiqah), then it is accepted. If he is not trustworthy, then the greater opinion (akbar al-ra'y) is considered. And if he has no opinion, he should not buy it because of the presence of an obstacle, so a proof is necessary. Translation: And if the one who brought her is a male slave or female slave, he should not accept her nor buy her until he asks, because the slave has no ownership, so it is known that the ownership belongs to someone else. Then if he informed him that his master gave him permission and he is trustworthy, then it is accepted. If he is not trustworthy, then reliance is on overwhelming presumption (ghalib al-zann). And if he has no opinion, then he should not buy it due to the presence of an obstacle, so some proof is necessary. Explanation: If a male slave or female slave brings a slave-girl for sale, it is apparent that the slave cannot own this slave-girl; rather it is someone else's property. So without investigation, no transaction should be made: do not buy it and do not accept it as a gift without investigation. Yes, upon asking, if he says that my master permitted me this transaction and the informant is trustworthy, then his word is accepted. If he is not trustworthy, then overwhelming presumption is considered. And if the buyer has no established opinion, but rather doubt and uncertainty, then he should not buy because there is an obstacle, and that is that the seller is a slave owned by someone else. So until a proof is obtained, he should not buy. The proof, in the case of a trustworthy informant, is his news; in the case of an untrustworthy informant, it is overwhelming presumption.
Source pages p. 370

Commentary
This passage deals with the situation where a slave (male or female) brings a slave-girl for sale. Since a slave cannot own property in Hanafi law, it is immediately apparent that ownership lies with the master. Therefore, the buyer must investigate before any transaction. He should not accept the slave-girl as a gift or purchase her without inquiry. If the slave claims that his master gave him permission, the buyer must assess the credibility of the informant. If the slave is trustworthy (thiqah), his statement is accepted. If he is not trustworthy, the buyer must rely on his own overwhelming presumption (ghalib al-zann). If the buyer has no formed opinion (ra'y) and remains in doubt, he must refrain from buying because a clear obstacle (hajiz) exists: the seller's lack of ownership capacity. A proof (dalil) is needed to overcome this obstacle. The proof is either the trustworthy informant's news or the buyer's overwhelming presumption based on circumstances. This ruling emphasizes the importance of verifying ownership when dealing with slaves, who are not independent legal persons in property matters.

Key Terms
مملوک (mamluk): A male or female slave who cannot own property in Hanafi law.
ثقہ (thiqah): A trustworthy person whose statements are accepted without further verification.
ظن غالب (ghalib al-zann): Overwhelming presumption that guides decision-making when certain knowledge is unavailable.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The age of the slave-girl.
B) Whether the slave has permission from his master.
C) The price is fair and reasonable.
D) Whether the slave is honest in his personal life.

Key Takeaway
A buyer must verify permission when buying from a slave, accepting his word only if trustworthy or supported by overwhelming presumption; otherwise, refrain.

Wine as Property: Muslim vs. Dhimmi Sales and Debt Collection
Ruling on debt payment from wine sale proceeds

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on taking the price of wine as debt payment, distinguish between a Muslim and a dhimmi seller, and explain the underlying principle of mal mutaqawwim.

Core Idea
Wine is not property for Muslims, so its sale is void; it is property for dhimmis, so their sale is valid.

This passage from Al-Jami' al-Saghir establishes a key distinction in Hanafi fiqh: the concept of valuable property (mal mutaqawwim) varies based on legal capacity. Wine is not considered valuable property for a Muslim due to its prohibition, but it is for a dhimmi who may lawfully own it. From this principle, we derive a practical ruling regarding debt collection from wine sales.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
State the two cases presented: a Muslim seller and a dhimmi seller, both having sold wine and taken its price.
The seller's religious status is the key variable.
2
Determine the ruling for each scenario
For the Muslim seller: it is disliked (makruh) for the creditor to take the price. For the dhimmi seller: there is no harm (ja'iz).
The ruling is based on the validity of the sale.
3
Identify the underlying principle
Wine is not mal mutaqawwim for Muslims, but is for dhimmis. This determines whether the sale is valid or void.
Mal mutaqawwim: legally recognized property that can be owned and sold.
4
Apply the principle to each case
For Muslims: sale void → price remains buyer's property → seller does not own it → creditor cannot take it. For dhimmis: sale valid → seller owns price → creditor can take it.
The consequence follows directly from ownership.
5
Derive the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference is that a creditor of a Muslim seller must avoid the wine price, while a creditor of a dhimmi seller may accept it.
This distinction is unique to the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شراب مسلمان کے حق میں مال متقوم نہیں ذمیوں کے حق میں مال متقوم ہے اس قاعدہ پر متفرع ہونے والا مسئلہ قال واذا باع المسلم خمر اواخذ ثمنها وعليه دين فانه يكره لصاحب الدين ان ياخذ منه وان كان البائع نصرانيا فلا باس به والفرق ان البيع فى الوجه الاول قد بطل لان الخمر ليس بمال متقوم في حق المسلم . فبقى الثمن على ملك المشترى فلا يحل اخذه من البائع وفى الوجه الثانى صح البيع لانه مال متقوم في حق الذمي فملكه البائع فيحل الأخذ منه. ترجمہ محمد نے جامع صغیر میں فرمایا اور جب مسلمان نے شراب بیچی اور اس کا شمن لے لیا اور اس پر قرض ہے تو قرض والے کے لئے مکروہ ہے کہ اس سے یہ لے اور اگر بائع نصرانی ہو تو اس میں کوئی حرج نہیں ہے اور فرق یہ ہے کہ بیچ پہلی صورت میں باطل ہو گئی اس لئے کہ شراب مسلمان کے حق میں مال متقوم نہیں ہے تو شمن مشتری کی ملک پر باقی رہا تو بائع سے اس کا لینا حلال نہیں ہے اور دوسری صورت
Wine is not valuable property for a Muslim, but it is valuable property for dhimmis. A sub-issue derived from this rule: He said: When a Muslim sells wine and takes its price, and he has a debt, then it is disliked for the creditor to take it from him. But if the seller is a Christian, there is no harm. The difference is that the sale in the first case is void because wine is not valuable property in the right of a Muslim; thus the price remains the property of the buyer, so it is not permissible to take it from the seller. In the second case, the sale is valid because it is valuable property in the right of the dhimmi, so the seller owns it, and it is permissible to take from him. Translation: Muhammad said in al-Jami' al-Saghir: When a Muslim sells wine and takes its price, and he has a debt, then it is disliked for the creditor to take it from the seller. But if the seller is a Christian, there is no harm. The difference is that the sale in the first case is void because wine is not valuable property for a Muslim, so the price remains the property of the buyer, so taking it from the seller is not halal. In the second case, the sale is valid because it is valuable property for the dhimmi, so the seller owns it, and taking from him is permissible.
Source pages p. 374

Commentary
This passage presents a rule regarding wine and its legal status as property. The principle: wine is not considered valuable property (mal mutaqawwim) for a Muslim, but it is considered valuable property for a non-Muslim dhimmi. Based on this, a sub-issue is derived from Imam Muhammad's al-Jami' al-Saghir. If a Muslim sells wine and takes its price, and then has a debt, it is disliked (makruh) for the creditor to take that price as payment because the sale is void. The price remains the property of the buyer; the seller never lawfully owned it. Therefore, using that money to pay a debt is not permissible. Conversely, if the seller is a Christian (dhimmi), there is no harm in the creditor taking the price because the sale is valid. Wine is valuable property for a dhimmi, so the sale is valid, and the seller owns the price. The key distinction is that wine is considered property (mal) for dhimmis but not for Muslims, as their legal capacity to own wine differs. This ruling is based on the Hanafi legal principle that Muslims cannot own impure substances like wine as valuable property.

Key Terms
مال متقوم (mal mutaqawwim): Valuable property that is legally recognized in Islamic law; property that can be owned, sold, and compensated for.
ذمی (dhimmi): A non-Muslim subject living in an Islamic state under protection of the state, enjoying certain rights and obligations.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The legal capacity of property (mal mutaqawwim) depends on the person's status: wine is property for dhimmis but not for Muslims, affecting the validity of sales and the permissibility of taking its price.

Wine Sale and Creditor's Right: The Māl Mutaqawwim Distinction
Ruling on validity of wine sale and creditor's right, with proof via distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the creditor's right to take payment from a debtor who sold wine, and explain the distinction between a Muslim and a dhimmi seller using the concept of māl mutaqawwim.

Core Idea
Wine is māl mutaqawwim for dhimmis but not for Muslims; the creditor's right depends on the seller's ownership.

This lesson unpacks a nuanced ruling from Imam Muhammad's al-Jāmi‘ al-Ṣaghīr. A Muslim debtor sells wine and receives payment – can his creditor take that payment to recover the debt? The answer is not straightforward: it depends entirely on whether the seller is Muslim or dhimmi. The underlying principle is the Hanafi concept of māl mutaqawwim (legally valuable property).

Guided Path
1
Identify the core principle
The passage begins by stating a key legal premise: wine is not māl mutaqawwim for a Muslim, but it is māl mutaqawwim for a dhimmi. Understand this distinction – it determines the validity of the sale.
Māl mutaqawwim means property that can be legally owned and traded. For Hanafis, wine lacks this quality for Muslims because it is inherently impure and prohibited.
2
Analyze the first scenario (Muslim seller)
Imam Muhammad says: if a Muslim sells wine and takes its price, and he owes a debt, it is disliked (makrūh) for the creditor to take that price. Why? Because the sale is bāṭil (invalid) – wine is not māl mutaqawwim for the Muslim seller, so he never validly owned the price. The price remains property of the buyer.
The ruling is makrūh taḥrīmī, not ḥarām, because the creditor is acting to recover a debt but the money is not lawfully owned by the debtor.
3
Analyze the second scenario (dhimmi seller)
If the seller is a dhimmi, there is no harm (lā ba'sa) for the creditor to take the price. The sale is valid because wine is māl mutaqawwim for the dhimmi, so the seller owns the price. The creditor may take it.
The dhimmi's contract is valid; the creditor's claim attaches to property that the debtor legitimately owns.
4
Trace the distinction (farq)
The difference hinges on ownership of the price. For the Muslim seller, the sale is void → price belongs to buyer → creditor cannot take from seller. For the dhimmi seller, sale is valid → price belongs to seller → creditor may take.
This distinction is based on a rational proof (dalīl 'aqlī): the ruling follows from the nature of māl mutaqawwim.
5
Compare with other madhāhib
Note that the Shāfi'ī school considers wine as māl mutaqawwim for all, so the sale would be valid even for a Muslim seller. This is a legitimate ikhtilāf (disagreement) rooted in different definitions of māl mutaqawwim.
The Hanafi position is unique and grounded in the idea that ritual impurity prevents legal valuation for Muslims.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شراب مسلمان کے حق میں مال متقوم نہیں ذمیوں کے حق میں مال متقوم ہے اس قاعدہ پر متفرع ہونے والا مسئلہ
قال واذا باع المسلم خمر اواخذ ثمنها وعليه دين فانه يكره لصاحب الدين ان ياخذ منه وان كان البائع نصرانيا فلا باس به والفرق ان البيع فى الوجه الاول قد بطل لان الخمر ليس بمال متقوم في حق المسلم . فبقى الثمن على ملك المشترى فلا يحل اخذه من البائع وفى الوجه الثانى صح البيع لانه مال متقوم في حق الذمي فملكه البائع فيحل الأخذ منه.
ترجمہ محمد نے جامع صغیر میں فرمایا اور جب مسلمان نے شراب بیچی اور اس کا شمن لے لیا اور اس پر قرض ہے تو قرض والے کے لئے مکروہ ہے کہ اس سے یہ لے اور اگر بائع نصرانی ہو تو اس میں کوئی حرج نہیں ہے اور فرق یہ ہے کہ بیچ پہلی صورت میں باطل ہو گئی اس لئے کہ شراب مسلمان کے حق میں مال متقوم نہیں ہے تو شمن مشتری کی ملک پر باقی رہا تو بائع سے اس کا لینا حلال نہیں ہے اور دوسری صورت میں بھی صحیح ہو گئی اس لئے کہ وہ ذمی کے حق میں مال متقوم ہے تو بائع اس کا مالک ہو گیا تو اس سے لینا حلال ہے۔ تشریح مسلمان کے حق میں شراب مال متقوم نہیں ہے اور ذمیوں کے حق میں مال متقوم ہے لہذا کسی مسلمان کا کسی پر قرض ہوا اور وہ شراب بیچ کر اس کا قرض اداء کرتا ہے تو اب دو صورتیں ہیں، یہ بائع مسلمان ہے یا ز می اگر زمی ہے تو چونکہ وہ اس کا مالک ہو چکا ہے تو اس کو لینے میں کوئی حرج نہیں ہے اور اگر مسلمان ہے تو لینا حلال نہیں ہے کیونکہ مسلمان خود اس کا مالک نہیں بنا بلکہ اس کا مالک تو مشتری ہے اس لئے کہ لینا حلال نہیں ہے۔
Wine is not valuable property (māl mutaqawwim) for Muslims; it is valuable property for dhimmis. A ruling branching from this principle: He (Muhammad) said: If a Muslim sells wine and takes its price, and he has a debt, then it is disliked (makrūh) for the creditor to take from it. But if the seller is a Christian (dhimmi), there is no harm. The difference is that the sale in the first case is invalid because wine is not valuable property for Muslims, so the price remains the property of the buyer, and it is not permissible to take it from the seller. In the second case, the sale is valid because wine is valuable property for the dhimmi, so the seller owns it, and taking from him is permissible.
Source pages p. 374

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with a supporting proof-by-distinction (farq) regarding the sale of wine. The principle is that wine is not considered valuable property (māl mutaqawwim) for Muslims, but it is for dhimmis. The ruling cites Imam Muhammad's statement from al-Jāmi‘ al-Ṣaghīr: If a Muslim sells wine and receives its price, and he owes a debt, it is disliked (makrūh) for the creditor to take that money from him. This is because the sale is invalid (bāṭil) for the Muslim seller—wine is not a valid commodity for him—so the price remains the property of the buyer (the one who purchased the wine). The creditor cannot take what belongs to the buyer. However, if the seller is a dhimmi, the sale is valid because wine is valuable property for him; he owns the price, so the creditor may take it without dislike. The commentary explains that the distinction hinges on ownership: the Muslim seller never becomes owner of the price because the sale is void, whereas the dhimmi seller does own the price.

Key Terms
مال متقوم (māl mutaqawwim): Property that is legally valuable and whose sale and ownership are recognized by Islamic law; for Muslims, wine is not.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling for a creditor taking payment from a Muslim debtor who sold wine?
A) It is permissible (halal) because the debtor received money.
B) It is disliked (makruh) because the sale was invalid.
C) It is forbidden (haram) because wine is impure.
D) It is recommended (mustahab) to encourage debt repayment.

Key Takeaway
A creditor's right to payment depends on the debtor's lawful ownership of the proceeds; wine sold by a Muslim does not create lawful ownership for the seller.

The Ruling on Hoarding: When Harming the Public Triggers Dislike
Ruling on hoarding with hadith proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on hoarding (ihtikar) and identify the condition that triggers dislike, citing the hadith proof.

Core Idea
Hoarding is disliked only when it harms the public; in a large city with no harm, it is not disliked.

This lesson examines the Hanafi position on hoarding (ihtikar) of food provisions. The classical text from al-Quduri's Mukhtasar, commented upon in al-Hidayah, establishes that hoarding is not inherently prohibited but becomes disliked when it causes harm to the community. The ruling is supported by a hadith and an underlying rationale based on public rights.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Hoarding (ihtikar) and dealing with caravans (talaqqi) are ruled disliked (makruh) when harmful. State the act and its hukm level.
2
Locate the dalil
The Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'The one who brings goods (jālib) is blessed, and the hoarder (muḥtakir) is cursed.' This hadith is the primary textual evidence.
From Sunan Ibn Mājah.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The word 'cursed' (malʿūn) indicates strong disapproval. In Hanafi usul, such expressions can imply makrūh taḥrīmī or tanzīhī. Combined with the condition of harm, it yields dislike (makrūh tanzīhī) rather than prohibition.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Some schools consider hoarding unconditionally ḥarām based on the same hadith. The Hanafi school limits it to causing harm.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The relied-upon (muftā bihī) position is that hoarding is disliked only when harmful. This balances the hadith with the rationale of public harm and is the position of al-Qudūrī.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
انسانوں کے غلہ اور چو پاؤں کے چارہ میں احتکار مکروہ ہے
قال ويكره الاحتكار فى اقوات الادميين والبهائم اذا كان ذلك في بلد يضر الاحتكار باهله و کذلک التلقى فاما اذا كان لا يضر فلا بأس به والاصل فيه قوله عليه السلام الجالب مرزوق والمحتكر ملعون ولا نه تعلق به حق العامة وفى الامتناع عن البيع ابطال حقهم وتضييق الامر عليهم فيكره اذا كان يضربهم ذلك بان كانت البلدة صغيرة بخلاف ما اذا لم يضر بان كان المصر كبير الانه حابس ملكه من غير اضرار بعيره
ترجمہ قدوری نے فرمایا ، احتکار (غلہ کو مہنگائی کے انتظار میں روکنا مکروہ ہے انسانوں کی غذاؤں میں اور چوپاؤں کی غذاؤں میں جبکہ یہ ایسے شہر میں ہو کہ احتکار اس کے باشندوں کو مضر ہو اور ایسے ہی تلقی ، پس بہر حال جبکہ احتکار مضر نہ ہو تو اس میں کوئی حرج نہیں ہے اور دلیل اس میں فرمان نبی علیہ السلام ہے کہ جالب (باہر سے غلہ لا کر شہر میں فروخت کرنے والا ) مرزوق ہے اور مختصر مامون ہے اور ا سلئے کہ اس غذا کے ساتھ عام لوگوں کا حق وابستہ ہو گیا ہے اور بیچنے سے روکنے میں ان کے حق کو باطل کرنا اور ان کے اس پر معاملہ کو تنگ کرنا ہے تو یہ مکروہ ہے جبکہ یہ ان کیلئے مضر ہوں اس طریقہ پر کہ شہر چھوٹا ہو بخلاف اس صورت کے جبکہ مضر نہ ہو اس طریقہ پر کہ شہر بڑا ہو اسلئے کہ وہ اپنی ملک کو روکنے والا ہے اپنے غیر کو نقصان پہنچائے بغیر ۔
تشریح احتکار کہتے ہیں غلہ یا گھاس کو روکنا کہ جب مہنگا ہو جائے گا اس وقت فروخت کروں گا در تلقی کہتے ہیں کہ شہر سے اس قافلہ والوں سے ملنا جو غلہ اور اناج لے کر شہر میں آرہا ہے اور وہ شہر سے باہر ہی اس غلہ کو خریدے اور اس کا ارادہ یہ ہے کہ اس کو روک کر رکھوں گا اور جب بھاڈ بڑھے گا تب فروخت کروں گا۔ حدیث مذکور ابن ماجہ میں ہے جس میں جالب سے مراد وہ شخص ہے جو باہر سے غلہ خرید کر شہر میں لا کر فروخت کرے تو وہ مرزوق ہے اسلئے کہ لوگوں کو اس سے نفع پہنچے گا۔ تو اس کو مسلمانوں کی دعاء کی برکات پہنچیں گی اور مختمگر وہ ہے جو غملہ کو اسلئے خریدتا ہے کہ اس کو لا کر دوں گا اور اس سے لوگوں کو ضرر پہنچے۔ ملون دو قسم کا ہوتا ہے۔ اللہ کی رحمت سے دور ہونا اور یتیم فقط کفار کیلئے مخصوص ہے۔ ابزار اور مقام صائین سے دور ہونا۔ اور یہاں یہی معنی مراد ہے اسلئے کہ اہل حق کے نزدیک بندہ گناہ کبیر کے ارتکاب کی محدسے ایمان سے خارج نہیں ہوتا۔
Hoarding (iḥtikār) of food for humans and animals is disliked (makrūh). He (al-Qudūrī) said: Hoarding of provisions for humans and beasts is disliked when it is in a town where hoarding harms its inhabitants, and likewise meeting caravans (talaqqī). However, if it does not harm, there is no harm. The basis for this is his statement (peace be upon him): 'The one who brings goods (jālib) is blessed, and the hoarder (muḥtakir) is cursed.' Also, because the public has a right to it, and refusing to sell nullifies their right and makes things difficult for them. So it is disliked if it harms them, when the town is small. Contrast with when it does not harm, because the city is large, as he is merely withholding his property without harming others.
Source pages p. 375

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on hoarding (iḥtikār) and meeting caravans (talaqqī), with a proof from hadith. The matn from al-Qudūrī states that hoarding food for humans and animals is disliked when it harms the people of the town. Talaqqī (meeting a caravan before it reaches the city to buy all goods) is similar. However, if no harm results (e.g., in a large city where alternative supplies exist), it is permissible. The proof is the Prophet's hadith: 'The one who brings goods (jālib) is blessed (marzūq), and the hoarder (muḥtakir) is cursed (mal‘ūn).' The reason ('illa) is that when hoarding harms the public, it violates their right to affordable food, and withholding sale nullifies that right. In a small town, hoarding clearly causes harm; in a large city, it may not, so it is allowed. The commentary clarifies definitions: iḥtikār is storing goods to sell at a higher price later; talaqqī is buying from caravans before they reach the market to hoard. The hadith is from Ibn Mājah. The passage ends with a discussion on the types of curse (la‘nah)—one is being far from Allah's mercy, which even sinners can experience, and another is distance from specific places of worship (asnām?), but the relevant meaning here is the curse due to hoarding.

Key Terms
احتکار (iḥtikār): Hoarding food provisions to sell later at a high price, causing harm to the public.
تلقی (talaqqī): Meeting a caravan outside the city to buy their goods before they reach the market, often for hoarding.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under what condition is hoarding (iḥtikār) disliked?
A) Only in a large city
B) When it harms the inhabitants of the town
C) Only for human food, not animal feed
D) Always

Key Takeaway
Hoarding is disliked only when it causes public harm; the hadith is interpreted in light of the underlying rationale.

Ruling on Hoarding (Ihtikar) and Meeting Caravans (Talqi)
Conditions and ruling for hoarding and meeting caravans

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on hoarding, identify the condition of harm that triggers dislikedness, and apply the ruling to different scenarios.

Core Idea
Hoarding is makruh only when it harms the public; without harm, it is permissible.

The Hanafi school carefully balances property rights with public welfare. This lesson examines the ruling on hoarding (ihtikar) and meeting caravans (talqi) based on a hadith. The key is the presence or absence of harm to the community. Understanding the condition of harm will help you distinguish between lawful and disliked transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The text states that hoarding (ihtikar) in provisions of humans and animals is makruh (disliked) – specifically makruh tahrimi – when it harms the people of the town. If no harm occurs, it is not disliked.
The ruling is contingent on harm.
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
The proof is the hadith: 'The importer is blessed, and the hoarder is cursed' (al-jalibu marzuq wa al-muhtakiru mal'un). This is a hadith from Ibn Majah.
The word 'mal'un' (cursed) indicates strong disapproval.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah (how the proof yields the ruling)
The Hanafi school interprets 'mal'un' as a sign of severe dislike (makruh tahrimi), not absolute prohibition (haram), because the word alone does not prove haram without additional evidence. The public's right attached to essential goods supports the dislike when harm is present.
This distinguishes Hanafi from stricter views.
4
Note the condition – harm to the public
The text specifies that hoarding is disliked 'when it harms them, such as when the town is small.' In a large city where goods are abundant and no one is harmed, it is not disliked because the owner is merely detaining his property without harming others.
The size of the town is an indicator of potential harm.
5
Apply to meeting caravans (talqi)
The same detail applies: meeting caravans outside the city to buy goods before they reach the market is disliked if it harms the townspeople. However, if the one meeting conceals the city's price from the merchants, it becomes disliked in all cases due to deception.
Deception removes the benefit of the doubt.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
